    </div>

        <div class="tab-content">
            <a id="top-content-scroll"></a>
            <div class="tab tab-pane active" role="tabpanel" id="mainTabPanel" aria-labelledby="showFullText">
                <article class="article">
                    
                    
                        
                        
                        
                            
                            <div class="hlFld-Fulltext"><div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="contentNavigationDropZoneAbs">


    
    
    
    
    
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
            
            
            
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
    
    

    
    <div class="widget gql-content-navigation none  widget-none" id="2ffb43c7-15dc-4c79-9e29-8e4bed9b3d1e">
        
        

        <div class="wrapped ">
            
            <div class="widget-body body body-none "><div class="ajaxWidget" data-ajax-widget="gql-content-navigation" data-ajax-widget-id="2ffb43c7-15dc-4c79-9e29-8e4bed9b3d1e" data-ajax-observe="true">
    
            
            
            
            <div class="content-navigation">
                
                    
                        <button class="btn" disabled="">
                            <span><i class="fa fa-angle-left"></i><span class="bold">Previous</span> article</span>
                        </button>
                    
                    
                
                
                    
                        
                        
                            <a href="/toc/imhn20/44/9?nav=tocList" class="btn cn--tocLink"><span><span class="bold">View</span> issue table of contents</span></a>
                        
                    
                
                
                    
                    
                        <a href="/doi/full/10.1080/01612840.2023.2239916" class="btn cn--nextArticle"><span><span class="bold">Next</span> article<i class="fa fa-angle-right"></i></span></a>
                    
                
            </div>
        
</div></div>
        </div>
    </div>
    
</div><div class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1" id="S0001"><p class="first">We are living through the hottest July on record, and “perhaps the warmest human civilization has seen” (Snow, <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0011" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>, p. 24A). It is clear to me that the term “global warming” is obsolete. “Global boiling” is what we are experiencing, and the climate change deniers must be struggling to find a receptive audience when the dramatic evidence is all around us. Nurse authors Schenk et&nbsp;al. (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0010" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>) prefer the term “climate breakdown” rather than climate change, to heighten the urgency for nations (and individuals) to take action.</p><p>Brutal heat waves in Italy and Greece this summer (temperatures as high as 118F) caused archeological sites to close, tourists to flee wildfires, and governments to ban outside work in the afternoon (Gross, <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0004" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>; Horowitz, <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0007" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>). Residents sweltered indoors in homes without air-conditioning; only 19% of homes in Europe are air-conditioned. However, as Gross (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0004" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>) points out, supplying air-conditioning to additional buildings cannot be a sustainable solution to global warming. Using cooling devices causes increased fossil fuel burning and thereby increases greenhouse gas emissions.</p><p>In the United States, the city of Phoenix, Arizona, is experiencing the most consecutive days of extreme heat (110F or higher) ever recorded. In their article about homeless people in that city, Machowicz and Snow (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0008" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>) took readers into the world of one homeless man living in a makeshift tent. Mostly confined to his tent during the extreme heat, he struggles to breathe but knows that walking outside on asphalt streets is too dangerous (falling could result in third-degree burns). A survivor of childhood trauma and neglect, this man exemplifies the vulnerability of millions who are houseless or precariously housed.</p><p>The physical health consequences of extreme heat (such as heat stroke and even death) are well known. Media reports this summer depict the tragic toll of elders becoming dangerously dehydrated, agricultural workers collapsing in their fields, and babies dying in hot cars. Meteorologists on evening television, after showing us their fiery red maps, warn us to seek shade and air-conditioning as much as possible, drink plenty of water, and wear sunscreen. Schenk et&nbsp;al. (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0010" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>) provide a useful summary of the deleterious effects of higher temperatures on respiratory and cardiovascular diseases, diabetes, and other chronic conditions.</p><p>The mental health consequences of global boiling can also be devastating, although less well-known and publicized. An excellent report from the American Psychological Association (Clayton et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0002" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2017</a></span>) reviewed mental health impacts such as stress, depression, substance abuse, helplessness, and fatalism. New terminology also has been introduced in the literature. The president of the American Academy of Nursing (White, <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0013" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>) called special attention to <i>ecoanxiety</i> and <i>solastalgia</i>. In the APA report (Clayton et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0002" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2017</a></span>), eco-anxiety is defined as “a chronic fear of environmental doom” (p. 29) and <i>solastalgia</i> is defined as “the lived experience of negatively perceived change to home environment” (p. 27). The term <i>psychoterratic illness</i> is being used to refer to “earth-related mental illness where people’s mental wellbeing (psyche) is threatened by the severing of ‘healthy’ links between themselves and their home/territory” (Albrecht et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0001" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2007</a></span>). Terms such as eco-anger and eco-grief have also entered the lexicon to capture feelings regarding the profound loss of place when families lose forests, farmlands, and homes that have been theirs for generations.</p><p>Although people of all ages are distressed, a global survey of adolescents and young adults from 10 countries showed that 75% said “future is frightening;” 56% sense that “humanity is doomed;” and 39% are “hesitant to have children” because of global warming (Hickman et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0005" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2021</a></span>). British adolescents felt that climate change was out of control, and it was “too late” to avert the crisis (Thompson et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0012" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>). Youthful activists are discouraged by the inadequate response of adult leaders and governments.</p><p>What should mental health professionals be doing in response to global boiling? The APA report (Clayton et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0002" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2017</a></span>) provides helpful tips for mental health care providers who must (1) address eco-anxiety and depressive symptomatology and (2) promote increased resilience of both individuals and communities. Schenk et&nbsp;al. (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0010" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2023</a></span>) suggest several action steps for nurses to take, including education of individuals with mental disorders about the disruption of heat regulation during prolonged heat waves (a concern if they are taking tricyclic antidepressants or certain antipsychotic medications). Kenneth White, AAN’s president, calls for nurses to become involved in policymaking that addresses the health consequences of ecoanxiety and solastalgia (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0013" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>).</p><p class="last">As editor of a scholarly journal, I focus here on the need for careful concept analyses of the new concepts that have been introduced, especially those that possibly overlap, and for empirical research involving samples drawn from diverse age and sociocultural groups. Challenges for eco-anxiety research were outlined by Pikhala (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0009" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>). As is often in the case for researchers, the measurement of concepts is the Achilles heel. Eco-anxiety is a multi-faceted construct and therefore a challenge to measure, although two scales have been introduced (see Clayton &amp; Karazsia, <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0003" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2020</a></span>; Hogg et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0006" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2021</a></span>). Adequately measuring participants’ coping skills and emotion management skills is also imperative in eco-anxiety investigations. An item in one early scale, “I find myself crying because of climate change,” is interpreted by the tool developers as “bad,” but in fact crying could be a normal and healthy response to losses (unless prolonged, as in complicated grieving). Pikhala (<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0009" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>, p. 93) points out that adolescents may, in fact, be “better at crying about the sad situation than adults are, and better at feeling moral outrage.” Although a few qualitative reports have appeared (e.g. study of British adolescents by Thompson et&nbsp;al., <span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0012" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference" data-registered=""><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2022</a></span>), I see many opportunities for additional qualitative investigation using phenomenology, grounded theory, narrative, and ethnographic methods. I invite readers to submit reports on research, clinical interventions, and climate policy activism.</p></div></div><div class="pb-dropzone no-border-top" data-pb-dropzone="contentNavigationDropZoneFull"><div class="widget gql-content-navigation none  widget-none" id="d59557d1-e1fb-43ae-9542-192f8d2a53a7">
        
        

        <div class="wrapped ">
            
            <div class="widget-body body body-none "><div class="ajaxWidget" data-ajax-widget="gql-content-navigation" data-ajax-widget-id="d59557d1-e1fb-43ae-9542-192f8d2a53a7" data-ajax-observe="true">
    
</div></div>
        </div>
    </div></div><div id="references-Section"><div><h2>References</h2><ul class="references numeric-ordered-list"><li id="CIT0001"><span> Albrecht, G., Sartore, G.-M., Connor, L., Higginbotham, N., Freeman, S., Kelly, B., Stain, H., Tonna, A., &amp; Pollard, G. (2007). Solastalgia: The distress caused by environment change. <span class="citation_source-journal italicized"><i>Australasian Psychiatry</i></span>, <a target="_blank" href="https://doi.org/https://doi.org/10.1080/10398560701701288"><i>(Suppl. 1)</i></a>, S95–S98. https://doi.org/10.1080/10398560701701288  <div class="extra-links" id="e_1_3_1_2_1"><span><ul
